This was the first launch in the assembly of the International Space Station.\n\n## Specifications\n\n### Mass and Dimensions\n\nZarya has a mass of 19,323 kilograms (42,600 lb), is 12.56 meters (41.2 ft) long, and 4.11 meters (13.5 ft) wide at its widest point.\n\n### Module Description\n\nZarya, also known as the Functional Cargo Block (FGB), is the first module of the International Space Station. The Russian Zarya FGB space tug was the cornerstone of the new International Space Station since it acted as an adapter between the US and Russian-built ISS segments and also provided some propulsion and propellant storage capabilities.\n\n## Continuous Habitation\n\nAstronauts have continuously inhabited the ISS since Nov 2000, marking the beginning of sustained human presence in space. Once the station became habitable, early in 2000, three-person crews began living aboard and conducting long-term experiments.\n\n## Construction and Ownership\n\nZarya was built between December 1994 and January 1998 at the Khrunichev State Research and Production Space Center in Moscow, funded by a US$220 million NASA contract. The U.S.-funded and Russian-built Zarya, which means \"Sunrise\" when translated into English, is a U.S. component of the station, although it was built and launched by Russia.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9cddeb93-c694-4441-9006-a48c0f913782","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:47.674Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"995738ff-f455-4384-aec6-a72c97628d62","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

When Apophis flies by Earth in April 2029, OSIRIS-APEX will be following close behind, passing just an hour after the asteroid and performing a gravity assist to catch up to it a little over a week later.\n\n## Mission Rename and Background\n\nAfter visiting the asteroid Bennu and sending its Sample Return Capsule back to Earth, the OSIRIS-REx spacecraft had enough fuel left over to travel to Apophis. NASA extended the mission under a different name, keeping the OSIRIS part but replacing Regolith Explorer (REx) with APophis EXplorer (APEX).\n\n## Extended Mission Details\n\nNASA extended the University of Arizona-led mission so that scientists can study the near-Earth asteroid Apophis for 18 months after the asteroid's close approach to Earth on April 13, 2029. An hour after Apophis's dramatic close approach to Earth on April 13, 2029, the OSIRIS-APEX spacecraft will use Earth's gravity to put itself on a course to rendezvous with the asteroid to begin an 18-month campaign of investigation and discovery.\n\n## Scientific Objectives\n\nAfter gradually maneuvering closer, OSIRIS-APEX will begin mapping the asteroid and measuring its shape, mass, and chemical composition. The probe will fire its thrusters at Apophis to throw off some the outermost surface and reveal what is underneath. OSIRIS-APEX will begin a 1.5-year campaign to monitor the orbit of Apophis from up close, particularly focused on measuring how thermal radiation from Apophis affects its trajectory. TESS will survey 200,000 of the brightest stars near the Sun to search for transiting exoplanets.\n\n## Instruments\nTESS will be equipped with four wide-angle telescopes and charge-coupled device (CCD) detectors, with a total size of 192 megapixels.\n\n## Orbit\nIt was placed into a highly elliptical 13.70-day orbit around the Earth. More specifically, TESS uses a novel highly-elliptical orbit around the Earth with an apogee approximately at the distance of the Moon and a perigee of 108,000 km. TESS orbits Earth twice during the time the Moon orbits once, a 2:1 resonance with the Moon.\n\n## Mission Duration and Results\nAfter the end of the primary mission around 4 July 2020, scientists continued to search its data for more planets, while the extended missions acquire additional data. As of 6 January 2026, TESS had identified 7,821 candidate exoplanets, of which 720 had been confirmed.\n\n## Cost\nWhile Kepler had cost US$640 million at launch, TESS cost only US$200 million (plus US$87 million for launch).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"115b9dd8-336a-468a-963e-660a3d83cc28","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:32.969Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d0b361df-1a3b-42fc-b1e6-16855079c6e2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

eoPortal\",\"url\":\"https://www.eoportal.org/satellite-missions/psp\"}]\n\nBased on the web search results, here is comprehensive information about the Parker Solar Probe:\n\n## Launch Date\n\nThe Parker Solar Probe was launched on 12 August 2018 by Johns Hopkins University Applied Physics Laboratory.\n\n## Closest Approach and Distance Record\n\nOn 24 December 2024 at 11:53 UTC, PSP made its closest approach to the Sun, coming to a distance of 6.1 million km (3.8 million miles) from the surface. Its beacon signal was received on 26 December, showing that it had survived the passage through the corona.\n\n## Speed Records\n\nAt its closest approach in 2024, its speed relative to the Sun was 690,000 km/h (430,000 mph) or 191 km/s (118.7 mi/s), which is 0.064% the speed of light. It is the fastest object ever built on Earth.\n\n## 2024-2025 Mission Details\n\nLaunched in 2018, the spacecraft used seven flybys of Venus to gravitationally direct it ever closer to the Sun. With its last Venus flyby on Nov. 6, 2024, the spacecraft reached its optimal orbit. The spacecraft's next planned close solar passes come on March 22, 2025, and June 19, 2025.\n\n## Four Scientific Instruments\n\nIts four instrument suites characterize the dynamic region close to the Sun by measuring particles and electric and magnetic fields, and each was specially designed to withstand the harsh radiation and temperatures they encounter.\n\nThe four instruments include:\n\n1. **FIELDS**: The FIELDS instrument suite captures the scale and shape of electric and magnetic fields in the Sun's atmosphere. FIELDS measures waves and turbulence in the inner heliosphere with high time resolution to understand the fields associated with waves, shocks, and magnetic reconnection.\n\n2. **ISʘIS (Integrated Science Investigation of the Sun)**: Uses two complementary instruments in one combined scientific investigation to measure particles across a wide range of energies. By measuring electrons, protons, and ions, ISʘIS will understand the particles' lifecycles — where they came from, how they became accelerated, and how they move out from the Sun through interplanetary space.\n\n3. **SWEAP (Solar Wind Electrons Alphas and Protons)**: Gathers observations using two complementary instruments: the Solar Probe Cup, or SPC, and the Solar Probe Analyzers, or SPAN. The instruments count the most abundant particles in the solar wind — electrons, protons, and helium ions — and measure such properties as velocity, density, and temperature to improve our understanding of the solar wind and coronal plasma.\n\n4. **WISPR (Wide-Field Imager for Parker Solar Probe)**: The only imaging instrument aboard the spacecraft. WISPR looks at the large-scale structure of the corona and solar wind before the spacecraft flies through it. About the size of a shoebox, WISPR takes images from afar of structures like coronal mass ejections (CMEs), jets, and other ejecta from the Sun.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"4c64cb4a-935c-4bbd-826a-1c442cdea805","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:04.106Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"124d30dc-ab0a-4712-bd25-8f739b8b0a8c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

It was designed and built at Goddard Space Flight Center, with the laser generation and detection systems provided by Fibertek. ATLAS measures the travel time of laser photons from the satellite to Earth and back; computer programs use the travel time from multiple pulses to determine elevation.\n\nAdditional capabilities: ATLAS measures height by precisely timing how long it takes individual photons of light from a laser to bounce off Earth, and return to ICESat-2. ICESat-2's laser will fire 10,000 times in one second. The original ICESat fired 40 times a second.\n\n## Orbit\nICESat-2 orbits in a near-circular, near-polar orbit with an altitude of approximately 496 km (308 mi). ICESat-2 will zoom above the planet at 7 km per second (4.3 miles per second), completing an orbit around Earth in 90 minutes.\n\n## Technical Specifications\nIt was designed to operate for three years and carry enough propellant for seven years. The ATLAS instrument will measure ice with a laser that shines at 532 nanometers – a bright green on the visible spectrum.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"821d8c16-d834-466b-9acd-81cc3d949e5a","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:17.640Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"454f222d-bd1f-4fff-ab47-bcb457a2c1ae","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## Launch Date and Impact\n\nThe DART spacecraft was launched on November 24, 2021, and collided with Dimorphos on September 26, 2022, at 14,000 mph (23,000 km/h) about 6.8 million miles from Earth.\n\n## Mission Target and Objectives\n\nDART is NASA's demonstration of kinetic impactor technology and the first-ever space mission to demonstrate asteroid deflection by kinetic impactor. The system is composed of Didymos (diameter: 760 meters) and the smaller moonlet Dimorphos (diameter: 150 meters), which orbits the larger asteroid.\n\n## Impact Results\n\nPrior to DART's impact, Dimorphos orbited Didymos once every 11 hours and 55 minutes, and DART's hypervelocity impact shortened this time to 11 hours and 23 minutes – a difference of 32 minutes. This deflection is the result of DART's kinetic impact and the \"ejecta\" – the many tons of asteroidal rock displaced into space by the impact, with recoil from this blast of debris substantially enhancing DART's push against Dimorphos.\n\n## Spacecraft Details\n\nThe 1,260-pound (570-kilogram) box-shaped spacecraft was guided to Dimorphos at roughly 14,000 miles per hour through the final 56,000 miles of space. The mass of Dimorphos is estimated as roughly 5 billion kilograms.\n\n## Companion Mission\n\nThe Italian Space Agency contributed a secondary spacecraft called LICIACube (Light Italian CubeSat for Imaging of Asteroids), which separated on 11 September 2022, 15 days before impact.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9920e8bc-948d-4835-8b12-3a34419961f0","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:31.153Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e389b570-a4e0-4c40-9840-bcc50c68a7e1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The mirror has a polished area of 26.3 m² (283 ft²), of which 0.9 m² (9.7 ft²) is obscured by the secondary support struts, giving a total collecting area of 25.4 m² (273 ft²).\n\n## Scientific Instruments\n\nThe James Webb Space Telescope (JWST) science instrument suite consists of four instruments and offers multiple observing modes. These instruments are:\n\n- Near-Infrared Camera (NIRCam) - provided by the University of Arizona, Near-Infrared Spectrograph (NIRSpec) - provided by ESA, with components provided by NASA/GSFC, and Mid-Infrared Instrument (MIRI) - provided by the European Consortium with the European Space Agency (ESA), and by the NASA Jet Propulsion Laboratory (JPL)\n- Fine Guidance Sensor Tunable Filter Imager (FGS-TFI) - provided by the Canadian Space Agency.\n\n## Sunshield\n\nWebb deployed a tennis-court sized Sunshield made of five thin layers of Kapton E with aluminum and doped-silicon coatings to reflect the sun's heat back into space. The sunshield reduces the temperature between the hot and cold side of the spacecraft by almost 600 degrees Fahrenheit in the span of its 5 layer, 4.8m height - from approximately 185F (85C) on the hot side to approximately -388F (-233C) on the cold side.\n\n## Orbit Specifications\n\nIt does not orbit around the Earth like the Hubble Space Telescope, it orbits the Sun 1.5 million kilometers (1 million miles) away from the Earth at what is called the second Lagrange point or L2. An L2 orbit is unstable, so JWST needs to use propellant to maintain its halo orbit around L2 (known as station-keeping) to prevent the telescope from drifting away from its orbital position. It was designed to carry enough propellant for 10 years, but the precision of the Ariane 5 launch and the first midcourse correction were credited with saving enough onboard fuel that JWST may be able to maintain its orbit for around 20 years instead.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"bf243e9d-f46e-4161-83d1-7c74cbf38d47","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:45.452Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"2ee10696-c8fd-4fef-8625-04344bf9377a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

This included letting all instruments cool down, calibrating the mirrors and instruments, and other procedures. The JWST completed its commissioning and began full scientific operations on 11 July 2022.\n\n## First Images\nJWST's first full-color images and spectroscopic data were released during a televised broadcast at 10:30 a.m. EDT on Tuesday, July 12, 2022.\n\n## Key Specifications\nWebb has a 6.5-meter-diameter (21-foot) gold-coated beryllium primary mirror made up of 18 separate hexagonal mirrors. More specifically, each module uses a dichroic to observe simultaneously in both the short wavelength channel (0.6 to 2.3 μm) and long wavelength channel (2.4 to 5.0 μm).\n\n## Detector Configuration\n\nNIRCam is an infrared camera with ten mercury-cadmium-telluride (HgCdTe) detector arrays, and each array has an array of 2048×2048 pixels. The long wavelength arm has one array per side (two overall), and the short wavelength arm has four arrays per side, or 8 overall.\n\n## Field of View and Resolution\n\nThe camera has a field of view of 2.2×2.2 arcminutes with an angular resolution of 0.07 arcseconds at 2 μm. It consists of two, nearly identical, fully redundant modules, which point to adjacent fields of view on the sky and can be used simultaneously.\n\n## Observing Capabilities\n\nIn addition to imaging with a wide range of narrow, medium, and broad filters, NIRCam also offers wide field slitless (grism) spectroscopy and coronagraphic imaging modes, as well as time-series and grism time-series observing modes for high accuracy photometric monitoring and spectrophotometric monitoring, respectively.\n\nAdditionally, NIRCam is also equipped with coronagraphs, which helps to collect data on exoplanets near stars.\n\n## Sensitivity\n\nNIRCam should be able to observe objects as faint as magnitude +29 with a 10,000-second exposure (about 2.8 hours).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"ff91645f-8b63-49ee-8d19-7febce9836ee","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:11.584Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4834bc7a-f105-426d-8990-4100a955229d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

It offers high throughput single object spectroscopy through fixed slits, spatially-resolved integral field unit spectroscopy, and a powerful multi-object spectroscopic mode using a micro-shutter assembly. NIRSpec is capable of observing more than 100 objects simultaneously.\n\n## **MIRI (Mid-Infrared Instrument)**\n\nMIRI provides JWST observers with coverage of mid-infrared wavelengths from 4.9 to 28.8 μm. Imaging can be obtained with 9 broad-band filters covering the wavelength ranges from 5.6μm to 25.5 μm, while spectroscopy can be obtained with a low spectral resolving power mode from 5 to 12 μm that includes both slitted and slitless options, or with a medium spectral resolving power integral field unit from 4.9 μm to 28.8 μm. MIRI also contains one Lyot and three 4-quadrant phase mask coronagraphs that are optimized to the mid-infrared spectral region.\n\n## **NIRISS (Near-Infrared Imager and Slitless Spectrograph)**\n\nNIRISS provides unique observational capabilities between 0.6 and 5 μm that complement those available with NIRCam and NIRSpec. Its efficient, all-reflective design enables low-resolution, wide-field grism spectroscopy; medium-resolution grism spectroscopy optimized for applications requiring extreme spectrophotometric stability; aperture masking interferometry; and parallel imaging through filters matched to those available with NIRCam.\n\n## **FGS (Fine Guidance Sensor)**\n\nThe FGS is housed in the same assembly as NIRISS and is a camera system designed to make sure Webb is stable and pointing in exactly the right direction throughout the observation. The FGS detects and identifies guide stars and ensures that Webb is locked onto those stars for the entire observation.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0f35d168-a976-418e-bfd1-8ca1f93e9cf3","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:24.495Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"97addc8a-a391-49e0-8cbf-5e5f4d52b3da","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The rover took 29 weeks to travel to Mars and made its landing in Jezero Crater on February 18, 2021.\n\n## Mass and Specifications\nThe rover weighs about 2,260 pounds (1,025 kilograms) on Earth, including the robotic arm with a 99-pound (45-kilogram) turret at the end, and about 866 pounds (393 kilograms) on Mars.\n\n## Instruments\nPerseverance carries seven primary scientific instruments, 19 cameras, and two microphones. The mission was launched at 5:34 a.m. EDT (09:34 GMT) on Oct. 16, 2021 from the Kennedy Space Center.\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nLucy is the first mission to explore the Jupiter Trojan asteroids. Over its 12-year mission, Lucy will explore a record-breaking number of asteroids: it will fly by three in the belt of asteroids that circle the Sun between the orbits of Mars and Jupiter, and then eight Trojans, which includes five asteroid targets and the satellites of three of those.\n\n## Trajectory and Gravity Assists\n\nLucy will fly by Earth three times to get a push from its gravity, making it the first spacecraft to return to the vicinity of Earth from the outer solar system. The second stage reignited to place Lucy on an interplanetary trajectory in a heliocentric orbit on a twelve-year mission to two groups of Sun-Jupiter Trojan asteroids as well as close flybys of main belt asteroids.\n\n## Asteroid Targets\n\nIn November 2023 and in April 2025 it flew by and photographed asteroids Dinkinesh and Donaldjohanson, respectively. Lucy will reach its first main target, the Jupiter Trojan asteroid Eurybates, in August 2027. A SpaceX Falcon 9 rocket launched IXPE from Launch Complex 39A at NASA's Kennedy Space Center in Florida.\n\n## Instruments\n\nIXPE is a space observatory with three identical telescopes designed to measure the polarization of cosmic X-rays of black holes, neutron stars, and pulsars. IXPE's payload is a set of three identical imaging X-ray polarimetry systems mounted on a common optical bench and co-aligned with the pointing axis of the spacecraft. Each system operates independently for redundancy and comprises a mirror module assembly that focuses X-rays onto a polarization-sensitive imaging detector developed in Italy. The Gas Pixel Detectors (GPD), a type of Micropattern gaseous detector, rely on the anisotropy of the emission direction of photoelectrons produced by polarized photons to gauge with high sensitivity the polarization state of X-rays.\n\n## Orbit\n\nIXPE was launched on December 9, 2021 into a 600-km circular orbit at approximately 0° inclination. The orbit hugging the equator will minimize the X-ray instrument's exposure to radiation in the South Atlantic Anomaly, the region where the inner Van Allen radiation belt comes closest to Earth's surface.\n\n## Spacecraft Mass\n\nIXPE has a mass of 330 kg (730 lb).\n\n## Mission Details\n\nIXPE is part of NASA's Explorers program, which designs low-cost spacecraft to study heliophysics and astrophysics. The estimated cost of the mission and its two-year operation is US$188 million (the launch cost is US$50.3 million).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"29352126-0de5-4905-8360-8a5842f7fdfb","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:33:05.484Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e2119510-3a4f-43f9-92bf-eaa301f17b81","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The orbiter is a 12-unit CubeSat.\n\n### NRHO Orbit Details\n\nIt entered NRHO around the Moon on 14 November 2022. This particular orbit—a near-rectilinear halo orbit (NRHO)—offers long-term stability with low propellant requirements for orbital station-keeping, by using a precise balance point in the gravities of Earth and the Moon that offers a stable trajectory.\n\n### Pathfinder Role\n\nAs a pathfinder for Gateway, a Moon-orbiting outpost that is part of NASA's Artemis campaign, CAPSTONE is helping reduce risk for future spacecraft by validating innovative navigation technologies and verifying the dynamics of this halo-shaped orbit.\n\n### Key Technologies\n\nThe spacecraft is also testing a navigation system called Cislunar Autonomous Positioning System (CAPS), which measures its position relative to NASA's Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter (LRO) without relying on ground stations.\n\n### Mission Status\n\nOn 18 May 2023, it completed its primary mission to orbit in the near-rectilinear halo orbit for six months, but will stay on this orbit, continuing to perform experiments during an enhanced mission phase.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"2d6fe025-c2ec-42d6-b2b5-87ca169c0ae0","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:33:17.974Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4da366c4-eed0-45a2-9589-b9ad0c592811","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

SLS and Orion launched at 1:47 a.m. EST, from Launch Pad 39B at the Kennedy Space Center. During this flight, the uncrewed Orion spacecraft launched on the Space Launch System rocket and traveled thousands of miles beyond the Moon, over the course of about a three-week mission.\n\n### Mission Timeline\n\nThe spacecraft entered the lunar sphere of influence on Sunday, November 20, making the Moon, instead of Earth, the main gravitational force acting on the spacecraft. On Monday, November 21, it came within 80 miles of the lunar surface, the closest approach of the uncrewed Artemis I mission, before moving into a distant retrograde orbit around the Moon.\n\n## Distant Retrograde Orbit (DRO) Details\n\n### DRO Characteristics\n\nDRO provides a highly stable orbit where little fuel is required to stay for an extended trip in deep space to put Orion's systems to the test in an environment far from Earth. The orbit is \"distant\" in the sense that it's at a high altitude from the surface of the Moon, and it's \"retrograde\" because Orion will travel around the Moon opposite the direction the Moon travels around Earth.\n\n### DRO Specifications\n\nIn the DRO, the Orion spacecraft will have an average distance of about 43,000 miles (70,000 kilometers) from the moon. The Orion spacecraft spent approximately three weeks in space, including six days in a distant retrograde orbit (DRO) around the Moon.\n\n### DRO Entry and Exit\n\nUsing the DRO for Artemis I requires the use of four major targeting navigational burns – two close and two far away from the Moon – to enter and exit the orbit. Orion will fly to its closest lunar approach about 60 miles above the surface of the Moon, then rely on the Moon's gravitational force together with a propulsive burn – known as the outbound powered flyby – to direct the spacecraft toward DRO where Orion performs a second propulsive burn to enter DRO and stabilize in the orbit.\n\n### Mission Achievement\n\nIt came within approximately 130 km (80 mi) of the lunar surface (closest approach) and achieved a maximum distance from Earth of 432,210 km (268,563 mi).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b5e59ed2-d1f5-4a24-85cb-f009e3704e5f","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:33:37.949Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"f25704f8-c6ea-4801-8668-f5d1a01955f2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The Block 1B rocket can send 38 t (84,000 lbs.) to deep space, including Orion and its crew.\n\n**Upper Stage:** The exploration upper stage has four RL10C-3 engines and four times more propellant and thrust than the Block 1's single-engine interim cryogenic propulsion stage (ICPS).\n\n## **Block 2**\n\n**Thrust:** The Block 2 will provide 9.4 million lbs. of launch thrust, compared to the Block 1's 8.8 million lbs.\n\n**Payload:** SLS Block 2 will be designed to lift up to 46 t (101,000 lbs.) to deep space.\n\n**Important Note:** On 26 February 2026, NASA announced that it would standardize to the Block 1 configuration and cancel development of the Block 1B and Block 2 upgrades.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"cd1010b5-0b05-41f9-9a59-dd65135bfef5","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:33:50.241Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b4521dbc-c3cb-4e3f-b02d-eec1e1e0b44d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The Orion spacecraft consists of a gumdrop-shaped capsule and service module, which together are about 26 feet (8 meters) long with a diameter of 16.5 feet (5 m).\n\n## Crew Capacity\n\nOrion has 50% more volume than the Apollo capsule and will carry four astronauts. The original launch window opened on October 10, 2024, but NASA announced that it would be standing down from the October 10 launch due to Hurricane Milton.\n\n## Spacecraft Size and Design\n\nIncluding its solar arrays, Europa Clipper spans 30.5 meters (100 feet), longer than a basketball court. Europa Clipper is the largest spacecraft built by NASA for a planetary mission.\n\n## Science Instruments\n\nThe spacecraft carries nine science instruments, and a gravity experiment that uses the telecommunications system. The instruments include remote sensing and in situ instruments and investigations, with imagers in visible, IR, thermal IR and UV wavelengths, radar in two wavelengths, radio and gravity measurements, a magnetometer, neutral and charged particle detectors, and a chemistry analyser.\n\n## Mission Timeline and Orbit\n\nThe spacecraft used a gravity assist from Mars on March 1, 2025, and will use a gravity assist from Earth on December 3, 2026, before arriving at Europa in April 2030. It will arrive at the Jupiter system in 2030, orbiting Jupiter instead of Europa to spend less time inside the planet's intense radiation field. It will orbit Jupiter, and conduct 49 close flybys of Europa.\n\n## Europa: An Ocean World\n\nBeneath a thick shell of ice, Jupiter's moon Europa hosts a massive ocean. NASA Science\",\"url\":\"https://science.nasa.gov/blogs/punch/2025/03/11/nasas-spherex-mission/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about the SPHEREx mission:\n\n## Launch Date\n\nSPHEREx launched on 12 March 2025 on a Falcon 9 Block 5 rocket alongside the PUNCH microsatellites from Vandenberg Space Force Base.\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nSPHEREx (Spectro-Photometer for the History of the Universe, Epoch of Reionization, and Ices Explorer) is a near-infrared space observatory that will perform an all-sky survey to measure the near-infrared spectra of approximately 450 million galaxies. Over a two-year planned mission, the SPHEREx Observatory will collect data on more than 450 million galaxies along with more than 100 million stars in the Milky Way in order to explore the origins of the universe.\n\n## Instruments and Specifications\n\nSPHEREx will use a spectrophotometer to perform an all-sky survey that will measure near-infrared spectra from 0.75 to 5.0 micrometers. The triple mirror telescope has an aperture diameter of 20 centimeters with a 3.5° × 11° field of view and six 2K × 2K mercury cadmium telluride (HgCdTe) photodetector arrays. Each 2K × 2K focal-plane array is covered with a linear variable filter, providing narrow-band response with a band center that varies along one axis of the array.\n\nThe mission will create a map of the entire sky in 102 different color bands, far exceeding the color resolution of previous all-sky maps.\n\n## Orbit\n\nOnce in polar orbit around Earth, SPHEREx will begin a two-year mission to create the first all-sky spectroscopic survey in the near-infrared. Circling Earth about 14.5 times per day, SPHEREx will complete more than 11,000 orbits over its 25 months of planned survey operations.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"27357f52-ad9c-4570-99d2-3794c1bdf477","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:34:41.735Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4eaf8d55-a48e-4aac-bea7-78932656d0b0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The twin spacecraft will study Mars' magnetosphere and how solar wind contributed to the loss of most of the planet's atmosphere over Solar System history.\n\n## Launch Details\n\nAfter several delays and revisions, based on the final mission plan, ESCAPADE launched to a staging orbit about a million miles away, curving around the Earth-Sun L2 Lagrange point on November 13, 2025. The spacecraft launched from Launch Complex 36 at Cape Canaveral Space Force Station, Florida. The ESCAPADE mission launched on Nov. 13, 2025 on a Blue Origin New Glenn rocket, sending two small orbiters to Mars to study its atmosphere.\n\n## Spacecraft Details\n\nEach identical ESCAPADE spacecraft has a dry mass of ~200 kg (440 lb), with a wet mass of 535 kg (1,179 lb). The spacecraft bus is based on the Rocket Lab Explorer spacecraft bus and is approximately 1.20 × 1.65 × 1.09 m (3 ft 11 in × 5 ft 5 in × 3 ft 7 in).\n\n## Mission Trajectory and Timeline\n\nIt will loiter there, studying space weather until late 2026, when the Mars transfer window opens. Then ESCAPADE will change course to dive within 600 km of Earth for a gravity assist and execute its escape burns to place it on a trajectory to Mars. The spacecraft is planned to enter Mars orbit by September 2027.\n\n## Magnetosphere Study\n\nWith different orbital periods (4.9 and 6.6 hours), the orbits will precess (due to Mars' non-uniform gravity field) at different rates and thus separate, allowing simultaneous measurements of distant parts of the Mars magnetosphere.\n\n## Mission Cost\n\nThe entire cost of ESCAPADE was $75 million, with $55 million being the cost for the spacecraft buses and science instruments and $20 million to cover the cost for the New Glenn rocket launch.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0869ce2a-5b67-42ac-92a8-f819f2bc5615","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:34:56.163Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"3129a82b-7ffd-4798-96ce-05ebad1dfd6d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The spacecraft lifted off aboard a SpaceX Falcon 9 rocket from NASA's Kennedy Space Center in Florida at 7:30 a.m. EDT.\n\n## Mission Overview\nIMAP is a heliophysics mission that simultaneously investigates two important and coupled science topics in the heliosphere: the acceleration of energetic particles and interaction of the solar wind with the local interstellar medium.\n\n## Scientific Instruments\nThe IMAP mission uses 10 scientific instruments to chart a comprehensive picture of what's roiling in space, from high-energy particles originating at the Sun, to magnetic fields in interplanetary space, to remnants of exploded stars in interstellar space. Specific instruments include:\n- Three energetic neutral atom (ENA) detectors—IMAP-Lo, IMAP-Hi, and IMAP-Ultra—that map the heliosphere's boundaries\n- Charged particle detectors including Solar Wind and Pickup Ion (SWAPI), Solar Wind Electron (SWE), Compact Dual Ion Composition Experiment (CoDICE), and High-energy Ion Telescope (HIT)\n\n## L1 Location and Operations\nNASA's IMAP reached its destination at Lagrange point 1, or L1, approximately 1 million miles from Earth toward the Sun on January 10. IMAP will be a Sun-tracking spin-stabilized satellite in orbit about the Sun–Earth L1 Lagrange point.\n\nIMAP began its two-year primary science mission on February 1 to explore and map the boundaries of our heliosphere — the protective bubble created by the solar wind that encapsulates our solar system.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8be1ad2a-f28c-4a0e-afa5-2c7600d9f2f0","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:35:09.489Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"8227605f-e236-4eb6-9cb6-c79d0aea6560","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The missions launched on a SpaceX Falcon 9 rocket from Space Launch Complex 4 East at Vandenberg Space Force Base in California.\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nPUNCH is a NASA Small Explorer mission that will make global, 3D observations of the young solar wind, from the outermost solar atmosphere to the inner heliosphere. PUNCH will be the first to image the Sun's corona, or outer atmosphere, and solar wind together to better understand the Sun, solar wind, and Earth as a single connected system.\n\n## Spacecraft Details\n\nThe PUNCH mission consists of a constellation of four small satellites in Sun-synchronous, low Earth orbit that together will produce deep-field, continuous, 3D images of the solar corona as it makes a transition to the young solar wind: from 1.5° (6 R☉) to 45° (180 R☉) from the Sun. Each of the four PUNCH spacecraft is just over a meter long and supports one instrument on the +Z deck (maintained toward the zenith in flight).\n\n## Instruments\n\nThe PUNCH satellites include one Narrow Field Imager and three Wide Field Imagers. The Narrow Field Imager (NFI) is a compact externally occulted coronagraph with a similar field of view to SOHO/LASCO-C3: from 6 to 32 R☉ on the sky. The external occulter blocks direct sunlight from entering the main optical aperture, which views the corona and starfield around the Sun using a compound lens system. The Wide Field Imager (WFI) is a wide-field heliospheric imager (HI) design based on STEREO/HI.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"90909cdd-6cd3-4d2a-8682-ff6b0b5c1923","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:35:22.514Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a5f785bb-a887-43b5-b6ab-4accd8542457","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Glover will become the first person of color, Koch the first woman, and Hansen the first non-US citizen to travel beyond low Earth orbit and to the Moon's vicinity.\n\n## Launch Date and Window\n\nApril 1, 2026, is its earliest potential launch date. Launch is targeted for no earlier than 6:24 p.m. EDT Wednesday, April 1, with a two-hour launch window.\n\n## Mission Profile\n\nThe crewed lunar flyby will be the second flight of the Space Launch System (SLS), and the first crewed mission of the Orion spacecraft. The mission profile is a multi-trans-lunar injection (MTLI), or multiple departure burns, and includes a free-return trajectory from the Moon.\n\nThe journey to the lunar vicinity is expected to last three days, and astronauts will spend one day in lunar observation of the far side of the Moon, with some parts seen up close by humans for the first time. An elevator on Starship HLS will be used to transport crew and cargo between the lander and the Moon's surface.\n\n## Design Characteristics\n\nThe Starship HLS is a modified configuration of SpaceX's Starship spacecraft, optimized to operate on and around the Moon, and the heat shield and flight control surfaces — parts of the main Starship design needed for atmospheric re-entry — are not included in Starship HLS.\n\nUnlike any other Starship variants, HLS will feature hot gas thrusters, which will be used during landing on the moon, because Raptor engines would kick up too much of the lunar soil.\n\n## Mission Profile\n\nA crew of two (or more) astronauts will transfer from Orion to HLS, which will then descend to the lunar surface for a stay of approximately seven days, including at least five EVAs.\n\n## Timeline\n\nFollowing testing, a crewed flight is expected to occur as part of the Artemis IV mission, no earlier than mid-2027.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"df376cfb-6570-4ba0-b8fb-3ef70f1be76b","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:36:04.435Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"25b396d7-80d6-4b57-8eb9-ba302f4d1cad","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The total value of the contract was approximately US$3.4 billion; Blue Origin stated that it was providing at least that amount of funding itself to the Blue Moon project. When fully assembled, it will be about one-fifth the size of ISS by volume and one-sixth by mass.\n\n**Orbit (NRHO):**\nThe Gateway was to be deployed in a near-rectilinear halo orbit (NRHO) around the Moon. The eccentricity of the chosen NRHO takes the station within 1,500 km (930 mi) of the lunar north pole surface at closest approach, and as far away as 70,000 km at its farthest point. Gateway will complete one orbit in about one week (6.5 days).\n\n## Key Modules\n\n**Power and Propulsion Element (PPE):**\nThe Power and Propulsion Element, PPE, is a 60-kilowatt solar electric propulsion spacecraft that will supply Gateway with power, high-rate communications, attitude control, orbit maintenance, and orbit transfer capabilities.\n\n**Habitation and Logistics Outpost (HALO):**\nHALO is Gateway's first habitation module — a pressurized command hub built to support astronauts living and working hundreds of thousands of kilometers from Earth. With NASA's Orion spacecraft docked, it will be able to sustain up to four astronauts for up to 30 days as they embark on, and return from, expeditions to the lunar surface.\n\n**International Habitation Module (I-Hab):**\nThe I-HAB would have a maximum launch mass of 10,000 kg and provide a habitable volume of 10 m3.\n\n## International Partners\n\nFive space agencies, including NASA, the European Space Agency (ESA), the Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency (JAXA), the Canadian Space Agency (CSA), and the Mohammed Bin Rashid Space Centre (MBRSC), are contributing to Gateway's assembly.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5c18de08-cd3f-4936-9d64-28a531005ff1","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:36:32.598Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"30047fa0-6ad5-459e-a8e9-fbd49ab7e0b6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The Nancy Grace Roman Space Telescope is a next-generation observatory due for launch no later than May 2027. The spacecraft is now fully assembled following the integration of its two major segments on Nov. 25, with the mission slated to launch by May 2027, but the team is on track for launch as early as fall 2026.\n\n## Instruments\n\nThe Roman Space Telescope is based on an existing 2.4 m wide field of view primary mirror and will carry two scientific instruments. The Wide-Field Instrument (WFI) is a 300.8-megapixel multi-band visible and near-infrared camera, providing a sharpness of images comparable to that achieved by the Hubble Space Telescope over a 0.28 square degree field of view, 100 times larger than imaging cameras on the Hubble. The Coronagraph Instrument (CGI) is a high-contrast, small field of view camera and spectrometer covering visible and near-infrared wavelengths using novel starlight-suppression technology.\n\n## Cost\n\nNASA announced that Roman would be launched on a Falcon Heavy launch vehicle, with a contract specifying readiness by October 2026 and a launch cost of approximately $255 million. Here are the key details:\n\n## Launch Date and Timeline\n\nDragonfly will launch on a SpaceX Falcon Heavy with a targeted launch period from 5 July 2028, to 25 July 2028. It is planned to arrive in 2034.\n\n## Mission Design and Specifications\n\nWith eight rotors, Dragonfly will take advantage of Titan's dense atmosphere and low gravity to fly between locations — traveling up to 110 miles (175 kilometers), farther than any planetary surface mission. Dragonfly is a car-sized, nuclear-powered rotorcraft being designed and built for NASA at the Johns Hopkins Applied Physics Laboratory (APL) in Laurel, Maryland.\n\nFollowing launch and a six-year journey to Titan, the Dragonfly rotorcraft will spend over three years investigating multiple landing sites across the moon's diverse surface.\n\n## Instruments\n\nThe mission includes several key scientific instruments:\n\n- DraGNS (Dragonfly Gamma-Ray and Neutron Spectrometer), consists of a deuterium-tritium Pulsed Neutron Generator and a set of a gamma-ray spectrometer and neutron spectrometer to identify the surface composition under the lander.\n\n- DraGMet (Dragonfly Geophysics and Meteorology Package) is a suite of meteorological sensors including a seismometer.\n\n- DragonCam (Dragonfly Camera Suite) is a set of microscopic and panoramic cameras to image Titan's terrain and scout for scientifically interesting landing sites.\n\n## Cost\n\nA revised cost projection was released in April 2024, with Dragonfly now expected to incur a total lifecycle cost of US$3.35 billion due to supply chain increases and delays caused by the COVID-19 pandemic.\n\n## Current Status\n\nOn 25 April 2025, Dragonfly passed its critical design review phase which marks the beginning of the full-scale physical construction and testing of the probe.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d63f2b52-5e87-4616-9fb8-55568e4eaaea","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:36:59.194Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6db63682-95e4-444f-a68d-19199c592451","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here's comprehensive information on NASA's Near Earth Objects survey mission:\n\n## Launch Date\nAs of February 2025, NEO Surveyor will be launched on a SpaceX Falcon 9 from Florida in September 2027, though as of December 2022, it was expected to be launched no later than June 2028. NASA's commitment to Congress in 2022 was that NEO Surveyor would launch by June 2028, but it's ahead of schedule with plans to launch in late 2027.\n\n## Mission Objectives\nNEO Surveyor is designed to help advance NASA's planetary defense efforts to discover and characterize most of the potentially hazardous asteroids and comets that come within 30 million miles of Earth's orbit. In the nominal survey the telescope is expected to detect two-thirds of asteroids with a diameter larger than 140 meters in the first 5 years.\n\n## Instrument Specifications\nNEO Surveyor consists of a single scientific instrument: a 50 centimeter (nearly 20 inch) diameter telescope that operates in two heat-sensing infrared wavelengths. It will use a version of the Astronomical Wide Area Infrared Imager (HAWAII) mercury–cadmium–telluride detector with a detector array of 2,048 × 2,048 pixels. The detector will be passively cooled to 30 K using techniques proven by the Spitzer Space Telescope.\n\n## Mission Operations\nNEO Surveyor will park itself at the Sun-Earth L1 Lagrange point, a spot 1.5 million kilometers (930,000 miles) away where the gravitational pull from the Sun and Earth balance. The NEO Surveyor spacecraft will have a total mass of no more than 1,300 kg (2,900 lb).\n\n## Development and Cost\nThe total cost of the mission is estimated to be between US$500 million and US$600 million, though the mission has a development cost baseline of $1.2 billion. The Jet Propulsion Laboratory will lead development of the mission.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"e9808da0-feca-442b-a12d-0406a4249e30","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:37:14.158Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"cd53e88d-bdef-4a59-8273-478f140a327a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

NASA Science\",\"url\":\"https://science.nasa.gov/astrophysics/programs/habitable-worlds-observatory/science-2/\"}]\n\nI've successfully performed the web search for the Habitable Worlds Observatory (HWO). Here are the key findings:\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nHWO was officially recommended in 2020 by the National Academies' Decadal Survey on Astronomy and Astrophysics. HWO is NASA's next flagship astrophysics mission after the Nancy Grace Roman Space Telescope (slated to launch by May 2027).\n\n## Primary Objectives\n\nHWO's main objective would be to identify and directly image at least 25 potentially habitable worlds. It would then use spectroscopy to search for chemical biosignatures in these planets' atmospheres, including gases such as oxygen and methane, which could serve as critical evidence for life.\n\n## Specifications and Technology\n\n**Mirror Design:**\nThe design for the HWO includes a 6–8-meter mirror; however, it would allow for a larger mirror if launch-vehicle technology allows by the time of its launch in the 2040s.\n\n**Wavelength Coverage:**\nThe Habitable Worlds Observatory (HWO) will be a large ultraviolet, optical, infrared space telescope. It will be the first specifically engineered to identify habitable, Earth-like planets next to relatively bright stars like our Sun with a coronagraph, and examine them for evidence of life.\n\n**Engineering Concepts:**\nEAC-1 is a 6m, segmented, off-axis telescope. EAC-2 is the same but uses a round keystone mirror and surrounding segments. EAC-3 is an 8m on-axis telescope with round segments.\n\n**Coronagraph Technology:**\nBuilding on innovations from the Roman Space Telescope, HWO's coronagraph will be even more advanced, offering a clearer view of distant worlds by blocking light from their host stars. The requirements for the optics within HWO's coronagraph may require corrections on the order of tens of picometers, smaller than the width of an atom.\n\n**Launch Vehicle:**\nThe HWO is designed to be launched on a super heavy-lift launch vehicle such as SpaceX's Starship, Blue Origin's New Glenn or the SLS.\n\n## Development Status\n\nAn official project office for the mission, the Habitable Worlds Observatory Technology Maturation Project Office (TMPO), opened at NASA Goddard Space Flight Center on August 1, 2024. NASA is expected to confirm the program – and its design – in the second half of 2026.\n\n## Two Design Options\n\n**Option 1 - Sky Crane Approach:**\nOption 1, with a projected cost of $6.6-$7.7 billion, is to use an upgraded version of JPL's sky crane that landed the Mars Curiosity rover in 2012 and Perseverance in 2021, with the sky crane for this mission having to be about 20 percent bigger.\n\n**Option 2 - Commercial Heavy Lander:**\nOption 2, estimated to cost $5.8-$7.1 billion, is to use a commercially-provided \"heavy lander.\"\n\n## Mission Timeline and Return Schedule\n\nBoth options envision launching ESA's ERO in 2030 and NASA's lander/MAV in 2031, with the samples returning to Earth in the 2035-2039 time frame.\n\n## Key Design Changes from Previous Plan\n\nFor both potential options, the mission's landed platform will carry a smaller version of the Mars Ascent Vehicle. However, in 2023, NASA announced that due to a shortfall in plutonium production, a mid to late 2030s launch would be more likely.\n\nThe Uranus Orbiter and Probe (UOP) study proposes a 2031 launch to take advantage of a Jupiter fly-by and utilizes a fully propulsive orbit insertion design with an Earth-to-Uranus transit times ranging from 13 to 15 years.\n\n## Mission Specifications\n\n**Spacecraft Configuration:**\nThe stacked flight system dry mass of 2756 kg includes 30% margin, with launch mass of 7235 kg. The orbiter is powered by three Mod 1 Next-Generation RTGs (NGRTGs), and is spin stabilized during most of cruise to reduce cost.\n\n**Cost:**\nThe Uranus Orbiter and Probe (UOP) is a Flagship-class mission concept ($2.15B FY25, Phase A–D, including launch vehicle) developed in response to the 2023–2033 Planetary Science and Astrobiology Decadal Survey.\n\n## Mission Elements\n\nThe orbiter would also deploy an atmospheric probe to characterize Uranus's atmosphere.\n\n**Science Phase Duration:**\nThe science phase would last 4.5 years and include multiple flybys of each of the major moons.\n\nThe mission deploys an atmospheric probe into Uranus shortly after orbit insertion, followed by multiple flybys of Titania to reduce the orbital inclination to the equatorial plane, carrying out observations of the planet as it does so. An equatorial tour of all five major moons follows, completing a 4.5-year science mission phase at Uranus.\n\n## Technology Considerations\n\nThe aerocapture maneuver would reduce the interplanetary transit time by 2-5 years (15-30%) relative to fully-propulsive orbit insertion. The Enceladus Orbilander would spend a year and a half orbiting Enceladus and sampling its water plumes, which stretch into space, before landing on the surface for a two-year mission to study materials for evidence of life.\n\n## Decadal Survey Status\n\nIt was proposed in the 2023–2032 Planetary Science Decadal Survey as the second highest priority Flagship mission, after the Uranus Orbiter and Probe. The selection was motivated by proposal's exobiology and planetary habitability focus.\n\n## Mission Architecture\n\nThe Enceladus Orbilander is envisioned uniquely combine both the functions of an orbiter and a lander into a single spacecraft. This design takes advantage of Enceladus's small size, the amount of energy required to land compared to capturing into orbit is negligible.\n\n## Scientific Instruments and Objectives\n\nOrbilander's notional 13 instruments are designed to (1) Search for life and (2) Determine the detailed habitability of the ocean by quantifying the geochemistry and understanding the geophysical processes.\n\nThese samples, both particulate and vapor, are then analyzed by the Life Detection Suite (LDS), a set of five instruments conducting complementary and orthogonal biosignature-seeking measurements.\n\nThe instrument suites include:\n- A Remote Sensing and Reconnaissance Suite provides contextual constraints from geochemical and geophysical measurements including the redox potential and disequilibria, salinity, pH, and temperature of the ocean, as well as interior processes that control water-rock interactions and vent ejection.\n- An In-Situ Suite images the surface for sample site selection and probes Enceladus' interior structure.\n\n## Sampling Systems\n\nAdditionally, three separate sampling systems are proposed for each stage of flight: a 1 square meter funnel to passively collect particles, a scoop for active particle collection on the surface, and a gas inlet for gas emissions.\n\n## Mission Timeline and Cost\n\nThe mission, with an estimated cost of $4.9 billion, could launch in the late 2030s on a Space Launch System or Falcon Heavy with a landing in the early 2050s. More specifically, The Enceladus Orbilander is proposed to be launched in October 2038. The spacecraft's proposed launch is timed so that a Jupiter gravity assist can be performed in October 2040 to save propellant for Saturn orbital insertion in August 2045.\n\n## Key Technologies\n\nTechnologies key to achieving the life detection science objectives include instrumentation matured under programs like COLDTech and ICEE-2, such as aspects of the sampling system and microfluidic devices, as well as well-known techniques like high-resolution and separation-capable mass spectrometers.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"04041014-84dc-4a4b-ae97-8def5b1b0458","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:38:16.141Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"875c452e-9f98-4840-be9d-2f4a124eb72e","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Some sources cite slightly different measurements: approximately 1,000 cubic metres (35,000 cu ft).\n\n### Modules\nBuilt by five space agencies — NASA, Roscosmos, ESA, JAXA and CSA — the ISS consists of 16 pressurised modules.\n\n### Total Construction Cost\nThe ISS has been described as the most expensive single item ever constructed. The Parker Solar Probe was launched on 12 August 2018, at 07:31 UTC.\n\n## Cost\n\nThe solar probe cost NASA US$1.5 billion.\n\n## Thermal Protection System Specifications\n\nThe thermal protection system is a little over 4.5 inches (11.4 centimeters) thick and has a diameter of about 7.5 feet (2.3 meters). InSight Mars Lander Specifications\n\n**Mass:** The lander mass is 358 kg (789 lb) including about 50 kg of science payload.\n\n**Cost:** The total cost was $830 million, increased from an initial $675 million after an instrument problem delayed the launch beyond the 2016 launch window.\n\n**Landing Site:** The spacecraft successfully touched down at Elysium Planitia on 26 November 2018.\n\n## RISE Instrument Details\n\nThe Rotation and Interior Structure Experiment (RISE) is a radio science experiment onboard InSight Mars lander that uses the spacecraft communication system to provide precise measurements of Mars' rotation and wobble.\n\n**Key Functions:**\n- RISE precisely tracks the location of the lander to measure how much Mars's axis wobbles as it orbits the Sun.\n- These observations provide new constraints on the core radius and help determine whether the core of Mars is mostly liquid, and which other elements, besides iron, may be present.\n\n**Technical Components:** The radio science equipment includes two medium-gain horn antennas (MGAs) on the lander deck, and an X band radio transponder (8 GHz) and transmitter inside the lander.\n\n**Measurement Capability:** RISE uses the spacecraft's radio connection with Earth to assess perturbations of Mars's rotation axis to within 10 centimeters.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your The $5 billion Europa Clipper spacecraft will first head toward Mars.\n\n## Instruments List\n\nEuropa Clipper has a powerful suite of nine science instruments that will work in sync while collecting data to accomplish the mission's science objectives. The instruments include:\n\n1. **Europa Imaging System (EIS)** - A wide-angle camera and a narrow-angle camera, each with an eight-megapixel sensor, that will produce high-resolution color and stereoscopic images of Europa.\n\n2. **Europa Thermal Emission Imaging System (E-THEMIS)** - A thermal imager that uses infrared light to distinguish warmer regions on Europa where liquid water may be near the surface or might have erupted onto the surface.\n\n3. **REASON (Radar for Europa Assessment and Sounding: Ocean to Near-surface)** - An ice-penetrating radar that will probe Europa's icy shell for reflections from the moon's suspected ocean and study the ice's structure and thickness.\n\n4. **Magnetometer** - The spacecraft's magnetometer is on a boom that extends from the spacecraft, reducing obstructions and magnetic interference from the spacecraft.\n\n5. **MASPEX (Mass Spectrometer for Planetary Exploration/Europa)** - Will analyze gases — both in Europa's faint atmosphere and in possible plumes. It will study the chemistry of the moon's suspected subsurface ocean, how ocean and surface exchange material, and how radiation alters compounds on the moon's surface.\n\n6. **SUDA (SUrface Dust Analyzer)** - A mass spectrometer that will measure the composition of small solid particles ejected from Europa, providing the opportunity to directly sample the surface and potential plumes on low-altitude flybys.\n\n7. **Europa-UVS (Ultraviolet Spectrograph)** - Will search for potential plumes of water vapor, and provide data on the moon's surface and thin atmosphere.\n\n8. **Plasma Instrument for Magnetic Sounding (PIMS)** - Will separate distortions due to charged particles from Europa's induced magnetic field, which carries information about Europa's ocean.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"3cab08aa-3b87-428f-9c28-62ed5b8313a3","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:39:38.434Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"091c148d-c391-4094-93f7-3e81133e68b2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

In late February 2026, NASA expedited Artemis III back to 2027, but it would no longer land on the Moon.\n\n## Mission Profile Changes\n\nUnder the revised roadmap, the Artemis III mission (2027) will no longer attempt a lunar landing. Instead, an Orion spacecraft will launch with a four-person crew to rendezvous and dock in Earth orbit with either SpaceX's Starship Human Landing System (HLS), Blue Origin's Blue Moon lander, or potentially both.\n\n## Key Test Objectives\n\nThis mission architecture is intended to validate critical technologies including: Docking and Proximity Operations testing the interfaces between Orion and the commercial landers, Integrated Life Support verifying air and water crossover systems between the vehicles, Propulsion and Navigation shakedown of the landers' maneuvering capabilities in a \"safe\" orbital environment, and Spacesuit Validation with initial microgravity tests of the new Axiom Space Extravehicular Mobility Units (AxEMU).\n\n## Program Restructuring\n\nThe most significant technical shift is the cancellation of the Exploration Upper Stage (EUS) and the associated SLS Block 1B configuration. NASA will instead \"standardize\" the SLS around the existing Block 1 architecture used for Artemis I and II.\n\n## Crew Details\n\nNASA will announce specifics on the Artemis 3 mission design and crew closer to the 2027 launch. Crew assignments have not yet been publicly detailed.\n\n## Future Lunar Landing\n\nBy shifting the lunar landing goal to Artemis IV in 2028, NASA believes it can execute a more sustainable long-term campaign.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"fa4bc8b0-554b-4a43-820d-a55bc8277f00","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:40:02.983Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4c9687b5-51d9-488e-ab5f-f6db04c1b4af","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

As of March 2026, launch is scheduled for early 2028.\n\n## Mission Profile\n\nThe mission depends on a prior support flight to place a lunar lander—either SpaceX's Starship HLS or Blue Origin's Blue Moon—into lunar orbit before the crew launch. When Orion docks with the lander the crew will transfer to it, descend to the lunar surface and conduct extravehicular activities (EVAs) there. They will then ascend back to the Orion waiting in lunar orbit, which will return the four astronauts to Earth.\n\nAs part of the Artemis IV mission, two astronauts would spend almost a week in the Moon's south polar region, where frozen water may exist in permanently shadowed craters.\n\n## Crew Details\n\nWhile Artemis IV crew details haven't been officially assigned yet, it is to be followed by testing of lunar landing equipment in Earth orbit on Artemis III in 2027 and then a crewed lunar landing mission on Artemis IV in 2028.\n\n## Gateway Specifications\n\nGateway is a small modular space station to be established in Near-rectilinear halo orbit (NRHO). Orion will transport the crew from Earth to the Gateway orbit, dock to the Gateway, deliver the I-Hab module to the Gateway, and return them to Earth.\n\n## SLS Configuration\n\nIt will be the first mission to use the standardized configuration of the Space Launch System (SLS), with a different upper stage in place of the Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage (ICPS) flown on the previous SLS launches.\n\nFor Block 1B the Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage used on SLS Block 1 will be replaced with the more powerful Exploration Upper Stage, increasing the rocket's trans-lunar injection capability from 27 to 42 t (60,000 to 93,000 lb). The spacecraft includes two single-axis solar arrays generating 400 watts of power.\n\n## Highly Elliptical Orbit\n\nTESS was placed into a highly elliptical 13.70-day orbit around the Earth. More specifically, its closest point to Earth is about 67,000 miles (108,000 kilometers), while its highest point is at 232,000 miles (373,000 km).\n\n## Orbital Characteristics\n\nIt achieved an orbital period of 13.65 days in the desired 2:1 resonance with the Moon, at 90° phase offset to the Moon at apogee, which is expected to be a stable orbit for at least 20 years. Data from the spacecraft is returned to Earth when the spacecraft reaches perigee, every 13.7 days.\n\n## Scientific Instruments\n\nThe TESS satellite uses an array of wide-field cameras to perform a survey of 85% of the sky. eoPortal\",\"url\":\"https://www.eoportal.org/satellite-missions/psyche\"},{\"title\":\"NASA Psyche mission: launch, path to the asteroid, science goals\",\"url\":\"https://www.skyatnightmagazine.com/space-missions/psyche-mission\"},{\"title\":\"Psyche | NASA Jet Propulsion Laboratory (JPL)\",\"url\":\"https://www.jpl.nasa.gov/missions/psyche/\"},{\"title\":\"Electric Propulsion for the Psyche Mission: Development Activities and Status | AIAA Propulsion and Energy Forum\",\"url\":\"https://arc.aiaa.org/doi/10.2514/6.2020-3607\"},{\"title\":\"Spacecraft | NASA Jet Propulsion Laboratory (JPL)\",\"url\":\"https://www.jpl.nasa.gov/press-kits/psyche/mission/spacecraft/\"},{\"title\":\"Electric Propulsion for the Psyche Mission\",\"url\":\"https://electricrocket.org/2019/244.pdf\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here are the specifications for the NASA Psyche spacecraft:\n\n## Mass\nAt launch, the spacecraft had a mass of about 6,056 pounds (2,747 kilograms), which includes the Deep Space Optical Communications (DSOC) technology demonstration. The dry mass is under 1400 kg, though another source indicates a dry mass of ~1650 kg. Excluding propellant, Psyche is expected to weigh up to 3,563 pounds (1,616 kilograms) at launch.\n\n## Dimensions\nThe spacecraft has two array panel wings, each with 5 solar panels and mounted with a single-axis gimble, which bring the total size of the spacecraft to 24.76 meters by 7.34 meters. The bus is about 16.1 feet (4.9 meters) tall, including the 6.6-foot (2-meter) booms for two of the instruments; 7.1 feet (2.2 meters) wide; and 7.8 feet (2.4 meters) deep.\n\n## Solar Electric Propulsion and Hall Thrusters\nIt has four SPT-140 engines, which are Hall-effect thrusters using solar electric propulsion, where electricity generated from solar panels is transmitted to an electric, rather than chemically powered, rocket engine. The thruster is nominally rated at 4.5 kW operating power, but it will also operate for long durations at about 900 watts. Only one thruster will operate at a time, providing up to 240 millinewtons of thrust.\n\nThe solar arrays produce 21 kilowatts of power when leaving the Earth and between 2.3 and 3.4 kilowatts of power during orbit around the asteroid.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"9428bf4a-d23c-49e2-9149-8a46d76c21d2","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:41:22.669Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"efda95f3-e337-4b6f-9f6c-0df0c4e1bac0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

spacecraft bus ~350 kg |\n| **Primary Mirror** | 6.5 m (21 ft) diameter, 25.4 m2 collecting area; 18 hexagonal segments of gold-coated beryllium (Be); each segment 1.32 m flat-to-flat, 20 kg |\n| **Sunshield** | 5 layers of Kapton E with aluminum and doped-silicon coatings; ~21 m x 14 m (tennis court size); reduces temperature from ~85 deg C (hot side) to -233 deg C (cold side) |\n| **Orbit** | Sun-Earth L2 Lagrange point, 1.5 million km from Earth; halo orbit; ~6-month period |\n| **Fuel Lifetime** | Designed for 10 years; Ariane 5 launch precision extended to ~20 years |\n| **Cost** | $10 billion total |\n| **First Images** | July 12, 2022 (public release) |\n| **Commissioning** | 6 months (Dec 25, 2021 to July 11, 2022); 29-day transit to L2; mirror alignment; instrument cooldown |\n| **Status** | Operational |\n\n### JWST Instruments Detail:\n\n**NIRCam (Near-Infrared Camera)** -- Principal Investigator: University of Arizona\n- Wavelength: 0.6-5.0 um (short channel 0.6-2.3; long channel 2.4-5.0 um via dichroic)\n- Detectors: 10 HgCdTe arrays, each 2048x2048 pixels (8 short-wave, 2 long-wave)\n- Field of View: 2.2' x 2.2' (two identical modules, adjacent fields)\n- Resolution: 0.031 arcsec/pixel (short), 0.063 arcsec/pixel (long)\n- Modes: Broadband imaging (29 filters), coronagraphy (5 occulting masks), wide-field slitless grism spectroscopy, time-series\n- Sensitivity: Magnitude +29 in 10,000-second exposure\n- Role: JWST's primary imager; also used for wavefront sensing during mirror alignment\n\n**NIRSpec (Near-Infrared Spectrograph)** -- Provided by ESA (components by NASA/GSFC)\n- Wavelength: 0.6-5.3 um\n- Modes: Multi-object spectroscopy (micro-shutter assembly with 250,000 individually addressable shutters, can observe 100+ objects simultaneously), fixed-slit spectroscopy, integral field unit (IFU) spectroscopy\n- Spectral Resolution: R ~ 100, 1000, or 2700\n- Detectors: Two 2048x2048 HgCdTe arrays\n- Role: Primary spectrograph for galaxy surveys, stellar populations, exoplanet atmospheres\n\n**MIRI (Mid-Infrared Instrument)** -- Provided by European Consortium with ESA and NASA/JPL\n- Wavelength: 4.9-28.8 um\n- Modes: Imaging (9 broadband filters, 5.6-25.5 um), low-resolution spectroscopy (5-12 um, slitted and slitless), medium-resolution IFU spectroscopy (4.9-28.8 um, R ~ 1500-3500)\n- Coronagraphy: 1 Lyot coronagraph + 3 four-quadrant phase mask coronagraphs\n- Detectors: Three 1024x1024 Si:As impurity band conduction arrays\n- Cooling: Active cryocooler to 6.4 K (coldest instrument)\n- Role: Only JWST instrument covering mid-infrared; critical for protoplanetary disks, high-redshift galaxies, exoplanet atmospheres\n\n**FGS/NIRISS (Fine Guidance Sensor / Near-Infrared Imager and Slitless Spectrograph)** -- Provided by Canadian Space Agency\n- FGS: Guiding camera for precise telescope pointing; locks onto guide stars; operates 0.6-5.0 um\n- NIRISS Wavelength: 0.6-5.0 um\n- NIRISS Modes: Wide-field slitless spectroscopy (R ~ 150), single-object slitless spectroscopy (R ~ 700, optimized for exoplanet transits), aperture masking interferometry (7-hole non-redundant mask)\n- Detector: 2048x2048 HgCdTe array\n- Role: FGS ensures pointing stability; NIRISS provides unique capabilities complementary to NIRCam/NIRSpec\n\n| **Notable Firsts** | Most powerful space telescope ever built; deepest infrared images of the universe; first direct images of exoplanets at these wavelengths; first detailed spectra of exoplanet atmospheres in mid-infrared |\n\n---\n\n## 1.179 — Perseverance / Ingenuity (Mars 2020)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | July 30, 2020, 11:50 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas V 541 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral Air Force Station, SLC-41, Florida |\n| **Landing Date** | February 18, 2021, 20:55 UTC |\n| **Landing Site** | Jezero Crater, Mars (18.4446 deg N, 77.4509 deg E) |\n| **Rover Mass** | 1,025 kg (2,260 lb) on Earth; 393 kg (866 lb) on Mars |\n| **Dimensions** | 3 m (10 ft) long x 2.7 m (9 ft) wide x 2.2 m (7 ft) tall; robotic arm 2.1 m (7 ft) |\n| **Power** | MMRTG (Multi-Mission Radioisotope Thermoelectric Generator), ~110 watts |\n| **Instruments** | Mastcam-Z (stereoscopic zoom cameras), SuperCam (laser spectroscopy/imaging/acoustics), PIXL (Planetary Instrument for X-ray Lithochemistry), SHERLOC (Scanning Habitable Environments with Raman & Luminescence for Organics & Chemicals), MEDA (Mars Environmental Dynamics Analyzer), MOXIE (Mars Oxygen ISRU Experiment -- demonstrated O2 production from CO2), RIMFAX (ground-penetrating radar) |\n| **Cameras** | 19 cameras + 2 microphones |\n| **Sample Caching** | 43 tubes total; 33 filled as of July 2025 (8 igneous, 13 sedimentary, 3 igneous/impactite, 1 serpentinite, 1 silica-ceite carbonate, 2 regolith, 1 atmosphere, 3 witness); 10 backup samples cached at Three Forks depot |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Seek signs of ancient microbial life; characterize geology/climate; collect/cache samples for Mars Sample Return; demonstrate ISRU (MOXIE) |\n| **Cost** | ~$2.7 billion ($2.4B build/launch + $300M operations) |\n| **Status** | Operational |\n\n### Ingenuity Helicopter:\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Mass** | 1.8 kg (4 lb) |\n| **Rotor** | Two counter-rotating coaxial rotors, 1.2 m (4 ft) span |\n| **Total Flights** | 72 flights over ~3 years |\n| **First Flight** | April 19, 2021 |\n| **Last Flight** | January 18, 2024 (damaged rotor blade on landing) |\n| **Total Distance** | ~17 km (10.5 mi) |\n| **Max Altitude** | 24 m (79 ft) |\n| **Max Speed** | 10 m/s (22 mph) |\n| **Status** | Mission ended January 25, 2024 |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First powered, controlled flight on another planet |\n\n---\n\n## 1.180 — Lucy (Trojan Asteroid Tour)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | October 16, 2021, 09:34 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas V 401 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral Space Force Station, SLC-41, Florida |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 1,550 kg (3,417 lb) wet |\n| **Instruments** | L'Ralph (MVIC visible imager 0.4-0.85 um + LEISA IR spectrometer 1-3.6 um), L'LORRI (panchromatic high-resolution imager), L'TES (thermal emission spectrometer 6-75 um), plus TTCam (terminal tracking camera) |\n| **Mission Duration** | 12 years (2021-2033) |\n| **Trajectory** | 3 Earth gravity assists; visits main belt then both L4 and L5 Trojan clouds |\n| **Targets** | Dinkinesh (flyby Nov 2023, discovered satellite Selam), Donaldjohanson (Apr 2025), Eurybates + satellite Queta (Aug 2027), Polymele (Sep 2027), Leucus (Apr 2028), Orus (Nov 2028), Patroclus-Menoetius binary (Mar 2033) |\n| **Primary Objectives** | First reconnaissance of Jupiter Trojan asteroids; study diversity of primitive bodies; constrain solar system formation models |\n| **Cost** | ~$981 million total lifecycle |\n| **Status** | Operational, en route (completed Dinkinesh and Donaldjohanson flybys) |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First mission to Jupiter Trojans; first mission to visit 8+ asteroids; first spacecraft to return to Earth's vicinity from outer solar system; discovered satellite of Dinkinesh (contact binary Selam) |\n\n---\n\n## 1.181 — IXPE (Imaging X-ray Polarimetry Explorer)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | December 9, 2021, 06:00 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SpaceX Falcon 9 |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39A, Florida |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 330 kg (730 lb) |\n| **Instruments** | Three identical X-ray telescope systems, each with a mirror module assembly + Gas Pixel Detector (GPD); ~6 days in DRO at ~70,000 km from lunar surface |\n| **Closest Lunar Approach** | ~130 km (80 mi) from Moon's surface |\n| **Maximum Distance from Earth** | 432,210 km (268,563 mi) -- farthest any spacecraft designed for humans has traveled |\n| **Orion Specs** | 5.02 m diameter crew module; heat shield rated to 5,000 deg F at 24,700 mph reentry; 4-crew capacity |\n| **10 CubeSat Secondary Payloads** | Including OMOTENASHI, EQUULEUS, ArgoMoon, BioSentinel, LunaH-Map, NEA Scout, and others |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Demonstrate integrated SLS/Orion performance; test Orion heat shield at lunar return velocity; validate systems for crewed Artemis II |\n| **Cost** | ~$4.1 billion (Artemis I mission cost); SLS program ~$23 billion through first flight |\n| **Status** | Mission complete (successful) |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First SLS launch; first Orion deep space flight; first spacecraft designed for humans to reach DRO |\n\n### SLS Specifications (All Blocks):\n\n**Block 1 (Current, standardized post-Feb 2026):**\n- Height: 322 ft (98.3 m)\n- Mass: 5.75 million lb (2,608 t)\n- Thrust: 8.8 million lb (39.1 MN)\n- Core Stage: 4x RS-25 engines (formerly SSME)\n- Boosters: 2x five-segment solid rocket boosters\n- Upper Stage: ICPS (Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage, single RL-10 engine)\n- TLI Payload: 27 t (59,525 lb) to Moon\n\n**Block 1B (CANCELLED Feb 2026):**\n- Height: 366 ft (111.6 m)\n- Upper Stage: Exploration Upper Stage (EUS, 4x RL10C-3 engines)\n- TLI Payload: 38-42 t (84,000-93,000 lb)\n\n**Block 2 (CANCELLED Feb 2026):**\n- Thrust: 9.4 million lb\n- TLI Payload: 46 t (101,000 lb)\n- Advanced boosters replacing SRBs\n\n### Orion Spacecraft Specifications:\n- Crew Module: 5.02 m diameter, 3.3 m length, ~8,500 kg\n- Total (with ESM): ~26,375 kg\n- Crew: 4 astronauts max\n- Duration: 21 days undocked; 6 months docked\n- Heat Shield: Avcoat ablator, rated 5,000 deg F\n- Service Module: ESA-built (European Service Module), solar electric power, propulsion\n- Reentry Speed: ~40,000 km/h (24,700 mph)\n\n---\n\n## 1.184 — Psyche\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | October 13, 2023, 14:19 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SpaceX Falcon Heavy |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39A, Florida |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | ~2,747 kg (6,056 lb) at launch; first map of entire sky in 102 color bands |\n\n---\n\n## 1.187 — EscaPADE (Escape and Plasma Acceleration and Dynamics Explorers)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | November 13, 2025 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Blue Origin New Glenn (inaugural payload flight) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral Space Force Station, LC-36, Florida |\n| **Spacecraft** | Two identical orbiters: \"Blue\" and \"Gold\" |\n| **Mass Each** | ~200 kg dry;
