each ~1 m long |\n| **Instruments** | 1x NFI (Narrow Field Imager, externally occulted coronagraph, 6-32 solar radii), 3x WFI (Wide Field Imager, heliospheric imager, to 180 solar radii) |\n| **Orbit** | Sun-synchronous, low Earth orbit |\n| **Imaging** | Continuous 3D polarized-brightness images from corona through young solar wind |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Track solar wind from corona to heliosphere; image CMEs in 3D; unify coronal and heliospheric observations; improve space weather forecasting |\n| **Cost** | ~$165 million |\n| **PI Institution** | Southwest Research Institute (SwRI) |\n| **Status** | Operational (launched March 2025) |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First mission to continuously image the transition from solar corona to solar wind; first 4-satellite coronagraph/heliospheric imager constellation |\n\n---\n\n## 1.190 — Artemis II\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | NET April 1, 2026, 22:24 UTC (2-hour window) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SLS Block 1 |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39B, Florida |\n| **Spacecraft** | Orion (crewed) with European Service Module (ESM-2) |\n| **Crew** | Commander Reid Wiseman (NASA), Pilot Victor Glover (NASA), Mission Specialist 1 Christina Koch (NASA), Mission Specialist 2 Jeremy Hansen (CSA) |\n| **Mission Duration** | ~10 days |\n| **Mission Profile** | Multi-trans-lunar injection (MTLI); free-return trajectory around Moon; lunar far side observation |\n| **Closest Lunar Approach** | Passes behind Moon with far-side observation |\n| **Splashdown** | Pacific Ocean off San Diego; US Navy recovery |\n| **Primary Objectives** | First crewed Artemis flight; validate Orion life support with crew; test crew interfaces; demonstrate MTLI trajectory; verify Orion systems for lunar missions |\n| **Status** | Ready for launch (crew arrived at KSC March 27, 2026) |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First crewed mission beyond LEO since Apollo 17 (1972); Victor Glover: first person of color beyond LEO; Christina Koch: first woman beyond LEO; Jeremy Hansen: first non-US citizen beyond LEO; first crewed lunar flyby in 50+ years |\n\n---\n\n## 1.191 — Artemis III (Restructured)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Planned Launch** | Mid-2027 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SLS Block 1 |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39B, Florida |\n| **Spacecraft** | Orion (crewed, 4 astronauts) |\n| **Crew** | Not yet assigned |\n| **Mission Profile (Post-Feb 2026 Restructure)** | LEO rendezvous and docking test -- NO lunar landing. Orion launches to LEO, rendezvous with one or both commercial landers (SpaceX Starship HLS and/or Blue Origin Blue Moon) |\n| **Test Objectives** | Docking/proximity operations with landers; integrated life support verification; lander propulsion/navigation shakedown; AxEMU spacesuit validation in microgravity |\n| **Key Change** | Originally planned as first crewed lunar landing; restructured Feb 26-27, 2026 to LEO test mission |\n| **Restructure Reason** | NASA standardizing SLS to Block 1; derisking lander operations before lunar attempt; safety-first approach |\n| **Status** | Planning/development |\n| **Notable** | Critical test before Artemis IV lunar landing; validates interfaces between Orion and commercial landers |\n\n---\n\n## 1.192 — Artemis IV (First Landing Under New Plan)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Planned Launch** | Early 2028 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SLS Block 1 (with Centaur V upper stage replacing ICPS) |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39B, Florida |\n| **Spacecraft** | Orion (crewed, 4 astronauts) |\n| **Crew** | Not yet assigned |\n| **Lander** | SpaceX Starship HLS or Blue Origin Blue Moon (pre-positioned in lunar orbit) |\n| **Mission Profile** | First crewed lunar landing since Apollo 17 (1972). Orion launches to lunar orbit; 2 crew transfer to lander; descend to south polar region; ~7 days on surface; ascend; return via Orion |\n| **Landing Site** | Moon south polar region (permanently shadowed craters with water ice) |\n| **Surface Activities** | At least 5 EVAs using AxEMU spacesuits; ~1 week surface stay |\n| **Gateway** | May deliver I-Hab module to Gateway station (if Gateway proceeds; as of March 2026, Gateway cancelled in favor of lunar surface base) |\n| **Status** | Planning/development |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First crewed Moon landing in 50+ years; first woman and first person of color to walk on Moon; first landing at lunar south pole |\n\n### Feb 2026 Artemis Restructure Details:\n- **Announced:** February 26-27, 2026 by NASA Administrator Jared Isaacman\n- **SLS Standardization:** Block 1 only; Block 1B and Block 2 development cancelled; EUS cancelled\n- **Manufacturing Cadence:** Target SLS launches every ~10 months (vs. every ~3 years)\n- **Gateway:** Cancelled March 2026; refocusing on lunar surface base (2029-2036), repurposing Gateway hardware and partner contributions\n- **Mission Reordering:** Artemis III becomes LEO test (2027); Artemis IV becomes first landing (2028)\n- **Dual Landers:** Both SpaceX Starship HLS and Blue Origin Blue Moon continue development\n\n### Starship HLS Specifications:\n- Height: ~50 m (165 ft, ~15 stories)\n- Propulsion: Hot gas thrusters for lunar landing (Raptors too powerful, would excavate regolith)\n- Features: Crew elevator for surface access; no heat shield or atmospheric flight surfaces\n- Contract: $2.89 billion (fixed-price with SpaceX)\n- Crew: 2+ astronauts for ~7-day surface stays\n- Refueling: Requires multiple orbital refueling flights before lunar transit\n\n### Blue Origin Blue Moon Specifications:\n**Mark 1 (Cargo):**\n- Height: 8.05 m; Diameter: 3.08 m; Mass: 21,350 kg fueled\n- Engine: Single BE-7\n- Payload: 3,000 kg to lunar surface\n\n**Mark 2 (Crewed):**\n- Height: 15.3 m\n- Crew: 2 astronauts for up to 30 days\n- Cargo variant: 20,000 kg (reusable) or 30,000 kg (expendable)\n- Contract: ~$3.4 billion (Blue Origin matching with own funds)\n- Support: Lockheed Martin Cislunar Transporter (tug + tanker for refueling)\n- First crewed mission: Artemis V (~2030)\n\n---\n\n## 1.193 — Nancy Grace Roman Space Telescope\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Planned Launch** | NET Fall 2026; no later than May 2027 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SpaceX Falcon Heavy |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, Florida |\n| **Orbit** | Sun-Earth L2 Lagrange point (quasi-halo orbit) |\n| **Primary Mirror** | 2.4 m (7.9 ft) diameter, 186 kg; same size as Hubble but lighter |\n| **Dimensions** | 12.7 m (42 ft) long; 4.4 m (14 ft) wide deployed |\n| **Mass** | ~4,166 kg spacecraft |\n| **Mission Duration** | 5 years nominal |\n| **Instruments** | WFI (Wide Field Instrument): 300.8 megapixels, 18 H4RG-10 HgCdTe detectors (4K x 4K, 10 um pixels), 0.48-2.3 um, 0.28 sq deg FOV (100x Hubble), 0.11 arcsec resolution; CGI (Coronagraph Instrument): technology demonstrator, visible/near-IR, starlight suppression for direct exoplanet imaging |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Dark energy/dark matter investigation (weak lensing, baryon acoustic oscillations, supernovae); exoplanet census via microlensing (~100,000 exoplanets predicted); wide-field IR survey; guest observer program |\n| **Cost** | ~$4 billion (+ $255 million launch) |\n| **Status** | Construction complete (Nov 25, 2025 integration); testing phase |\n| **Notable Firsts** | Widest field of view of any space telescope at Hubble resolution; first space coronagraph technology demonstrator; will discover most exoplanets of any single mission |\n\n---\n\n## 1.194 — Dragonfly (Titan Rotorcraft Lander)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Planned Launch** | July 5-25, 2028 launch window |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SpaceX Falcon Heavy |\n| **Arrival** | 2034 |\n| **Target** | Titan (Saturn's largest moon) |\n| **Spacecraft** | Car-sized nuclear-powered rotorcraft (octocopter) |\n| **Rotors** | 8 rotors (4 dual-rotor pairs) |\n| **Power** | MMRTG (Multi-Mission Radioisotope Thermoelectric Generator) |\n| **Flight Range** | Up to 175 km (110 mi) between landing sites (exploiting Titan's dense atmosphere 4x Earth's at surface, and 1/7 gravity) |\n| **Surface Operations** | 3+ years |\n| **Instruments** | DraGNS (gamma-ray/neutron spectrometer with pulsed neutron generator), DraGMet (geophysics/meteorology suite with seismometer), DragonCam (microscopic + panoramic cameras), DraMS (Dragonfly Mass Spectrometer for organics/biosignatures) |\n| **Landing Site** | Selk impact crater region |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Search for chemical biosignatures and prebiotic chemistry on Titan; characterize habitability of Titan's methane cycle; study atmospheric and surface composition |\n| **Cost** | $3.35 billion total lifecycle |\n| **Critical Design Review** | Passed April 25, 2025; integration/testing began March 2026 |\n| **Status** | Development (integration and testing phase) |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First rotorcraft on another world's surface (for extended mission); first nuclear-powered aerial vehicle on another world; first mission dedicated to Titan surface |\n\n---\n\n## 1.195 — NEO Surveyor (Near-Earth Object Surveyor)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Planned Launch** | September 2027 (ahead of June 2028 congressional mandate) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SpaceX Falcon 9 |\n| **Launch Site** | Florida |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | Up to 1,300 kg (2,900 lb) |\n| **Instrument** | Single 50 cm (20 in) diameter infrared telescope, dual-band thermal IR detection |\n| **Detectors** | HAWAII HgCdTe, 2048x2048 pixels; passively cooled to 30 K |\n| **Orbit** | Sun-Earth L1 Lagrange point (1.5 million km sunward) |\n| **Mission Duration** | 5 years primary survey; up to 12 years |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Discover and catalog 2/3 of potentially hazardous asteroids >140 m diameter within 5 years; characterize size, albedo, and composition using thermal IR; fulfill congressional mandate (George E. Brown Jr. Near-Earth Object Survey Act) |\n| **Cost** | $1.2 billion development baseline |\n| **Developer** | JPL |\n| **Status** | Development (on track for 2027) |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First dedicated space telescope for planetary defense; first mission specifically designed to complete the congressional PHO catalog mandate |\n\n---\n\n## 1.196 — HWO (Habitable Worlds Observatory)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Planned Launch** | Early-to-mid 2040s |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Super heavy-lift (Starship, New Glenn, or SLS-class) |\n| **Orbit** | Sun-Earth L2 |\n| **Primary Mirror** | 6-8 m (segmented; designs include off-axis and on-axis configurations) |\n| **Wavelength Coverage** | UV, optical, and infrared (UVOIR) |\n| **Key Technology** | Advanced coronagraph (tens-of-picometer wavefront stability); deformable mirrors; starshade possible |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Directly image at least 25 potentially habitable Earth-like exoplanets; spectroscopic search for biosignatures (O2, O3, H2O, CH4, CO2); general astrophysics (UV through IR) |\n| **Decadal Survey** | Recommended as #1 priority flagship by 2020 Astro2020 Decadal Survey |\n| **Technology Maturation** | TMPO (Technology Maturation Project Office) at GSFC, opened Aug 1, 2024; industry proposals selected Jan 5, 2026 |\n| **Design Concepts** | EAC-1: 6m segmented off-axis; EAC-2: 6m round keystone; EAC-3: 8m on-axis round segments |\n| **Heritage** | Builds on Roman Space Telescope coronagraph technology; JWST deployable mirror heritage |\n| **Estimated Cost** | $11+ billion (flagship class) |\n| **Status** | Pre-formulation (technology development) |\n| **Notable (Planned) Firsts** | First telescope specifically designed to find and characterize habitable Earth-like planets; most powerful UVOIR space telescope ever built |\n\n---\n\n## 1.197 — Mars Sample Return (MSR)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Architecture Status** | In formulation; NASA evaluating two competing designs; final decision expected second half 2026 |\n| **Option 1** | Sky Crane approach (upgraded JPL sky crane, ~20% larger than Perseverance); est. $6.6-7.7 billion |\n| **Option 2** | Commercial heavy lander; est. $5.8-7.1 billion |\n| **Timeline** | ESA Earth Return Orbiter (ERO) launch: 2030; NASA lander/MAV launch: 2031; samples return to Earth: 2035-2039 |\n| **Samples** | 30 tubes in orbiting sample container (from Perseverance's cached 33+ tubes at Jezero Crater) |\n| **Key Components** | Sample Retrieval Lander (SRL), Mars Ascent Vehicle (MAV, smaller redesign), Earth Return Orbiter (ESA), Earth Entry Vehicle |\n| **Design Changes** | Smaller MAV; radioisotope power replaces solar panels (survives dust storms); reduced complexity |\n| **Previous Cost Estimate** | IRB (Sep 2023): $8.4-10.9 billion; deemed not feasible at original architecture |\n| **Current Estimate** | $5.8-7.7 billion (depending on option) |\n| **Partners** | NASA (lead), ESA (ERO + sample fetch rover originally; ERO retained) |\n| **Status** | Formulation (awaiting architecture decision) |\n| **Notable (Planned) Firsts** | First return of samples from Mars; first launch from another planet's surface (MAV); first round-trip interplanetary sample mission |\n\n---\n\n## 1.198 — Uranus Orbiter and Probe (UOP)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Priority** | #1 Flagship in 2023-2032 Planetary Science Decadal Survey |\n| **Planned Launch** | Mid-to-late 2030s (originally 2031 for Jupiter gravity assist; delayed by plutonium production shortfall) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Falcon Heavy (expendable) or equivalent |\n| **Trajectory** | Earth-Jupiter gravity assist; 13-15 year transit to Uranus |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 2,756 kg dry (with 30% margin); 7,235 kg launch mass |\n| **Power** | Three Mod 1 Next-Generation RTGs (NGRTGs) |\n| **Science Phase** | 4.5 years at Uranus |\n| **Elements** | Orbiter + atmospheric entry probe |\n| **Probe** | Descends through Uranus atmosphere measuring composition, temperature, pressure, winds |\n| **Orbiter Tour** | Deploy probe shortly after orbit insertion; Titania flybys to reduce inclination; equatorial tour of all 5 major moons (Miranda, Ariel, Umbriel, Titania, Oberon) |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Characterize Uranus interior, atmosphere, magnetosphere, rings, and satellites; understand ice giant formation and evolution; probe atmospheric composition |\n| **Cost** | ~$2.15 billion (Phase A-D, FY25, including launch vehicle) |\n| **Arrival** | ~2044-2049 (depending on launch date) |\n| **Status** | Pre-formulation (technology development) |\n| **Notable (Planned) Firsts** | First dedicated Uranus orbiter (Voyager 2 flyby in 1986 was only previous visit); first ice giant orbiter; first atmospheric probe at an ice giant |\n| **Technology** | Aerocapture option could save 2-5 years transit time |\n\n---\n\n## 1.199 — Enceladus Orbilander\n\n| Field | Value |\n|---|---|\n| **Priority** | #2 Flagship in 2023-2032 Planetary Science Decadal Survey (after Uranus Orbiter) |\n| **Proposed Launch** | October 2038 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | SLS or Falcon Heavy |\n| **Trajectory** | Jupiter gravity assist (Oct 2040); Saturn orbit insertion (Aug 2045) |\n| **Mission Design** | Combined orbiter + lander in single spacecraft (Enceladus small enough that orbit-to-landing delta-v is negligible) |\n| **Orbital Phase** | 1.5 years orbiting Enceladus, sampling water plumes |\n| **Surface Phase** | 2 years on Enceladus surface, studying materials for evidence of life |\n| **Instruments** | 13 instruments total: Life Detection Suite (LDS, 5 instruments for biosignature detection), Remote Sensing and Reconnaissance Suite (geochemical/geophysical measurements), In-Situ Suite (surface imaging and interior structure probing) |\n| **Sampling Systems** | 3 systems: 1 m2 funnel (passive particle collection during orbit), scoop (active surface collection), gas inlet (emission gases) |\n| **Primary Objectives** | Search for evidence of life in Enceladus ocean material; characterize ocean habitability (pH, salinity, temperature, redox); understand water-rock interactions and vent processes |\n| **Cost** | ~$4.9 billion estimated |\n| **Arrival** | ~2045 (landing ~2047) |\n| **Status** | Concept study (pre-formulation) |\n| **Notable (Planned) Firsts** | First dedicated astrobiology mission to an ocean world; first landing on Enceladus; first direct sampling of extraterrestrial ocean material; combined orbiter-lander architecture unique in exploration |\n| **Key Technologies** | Microfluidic devices, high-resolution mass spectrometers, sampling systems matured under COLDTech and ICEE-2 programs |\n\n---\n\n## Summary Table: All Missions\n\n| # |
